RATING: 7
Straight out of the box and with no more than 3 players, Runebound is a playable game. But if you factor in the expansions and start playing with house rules (either solo or co-op) it actually shines.

RATING: 6
Not bad in any particular way; it just doesn't hold my interest. This game gives me a "so what" feeling. Nothing about the design brings you down into the gameworld; it feels like you're adventuring from an altitude of 50,000 feet. The individual elements (characters, monsters, combat system) seem fine, but there's nothing to combine them into a compelling game. 

EDIT: Rating changed from 5 to 6 after playing some expansions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's currently THE adventure/rpg game system available. It plays fairly smoothly (though the battle system seems a bit awkward). The graphics are very good. The flavor text on the cards is good. The components are of a high quality (cool "paint-able" plastic figures). There are expansions galore to make the game feel a little different from play to play. And the bonus is that the main game actually plays pretty well as a solo experience. 

The only real downside is the play time but there are plenty of official optional rules (and unofficial ones) that will speed up play. I'd never, ever play this game with more than 4 players as the game length would be intolerable. Though with 3 or less, this is a really good adventure game system.

RATING: 6
Runebound is a classic adventure game from Fantasy Flight Games in which mighty heroes must take on the perils of Terrinoth. The game can be largely played without conflict between the players but victory can only be claimed by the first player to defeat the Dragonlord Margath, so the players are actually in a race to level up and acquire powerful weapons, armour and allies in order to take on the final adversary.

RATING: 4

You don't feel like you are fighting a ranged combat followed by intense melee and sparkling magic - you feel like you are rolling against 3 numbers of on a card. It's just statistics. No magic, no fantasy, no nothing. 

I have played it a few time with different groups of players and so far noone I've played it with has asked me for another go. There are so many great games out there - skip Runebound!

RATING: 7
I was debating whether to wait and buy the new fantasy flight version of Talisman or this.  I bought this not having played it before and have no regrets.  It has all the same draw of talisman but with slightly deeper game mechanics in terms of movement and combat (though Talismans flavor cannot be duplicated). I expect and desire this type of game to take a long time to play.  That is not a negative at all for me.  However this game should be played with the quicker Runebound rules and the doom track as it does seem to provide the game with a reasonable pace while also encouraging players to take on harder challenges.  The game is very accessible as long as you have played through it once and is easy to teach.  I am definitely looking forward to what the expansions will add to this.  A new cornerstone of my collection that will probably get a higher rating with more plays. 
  
UPDATE: Bought some expansion decks and like how the game feels with the added content.  This game has a better fun factor than most. After repeated plays I find I really like the combat system in spite of its relative simplicity and the inevitable "push your luck" factor in combat is great at creating tense moments.  But without regular added content(which there is a ton of)staleness sets in rather quickly.  This is an ideal game for kids, they cried when I said I was thinking of selling it. Had to back off quick.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Originally, I got into the idea of trying Runebound 2 when I saw a review fawning all over it as a great adventuring game that feels like an RPG. I was galvanized to try it once Runebound 3 was announced and had apparently simplified the game and thrown out a lot of cool mechanics. What I would learn upon trying Runbound 2 is they were thrown out for good reason...

The multi-tiered encounter system is lovely. In fact, I am not sure how RB3 is going to make this work since it was so elegant here. Unfortunately, every encounter feels exactly the same. And that's all the game is: encounters. You move around, trying to kill as much as possible to buy things to kill more. Pretty typical RPG slog. 

Unfortunately, the combat just gets old so quickly. There are three phases, you roll dice, you use dice in the three phases, you repeat. There aren't many interesting decisions. You just sort of solve a mini-puzzle every turn (the same one) over and over forever and then the game ends.

This could be slightly enjoyable with someone at the table who adores it and is just incredibly enthusiastic all the time or who really gets into creating a narrative for everything. That's what we did for the first...30 minutes. After that, it felt really forced and the mechanics just stayed the same forever. RB2 is slightly boring, I don't think this will make it to the table much.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Meh. It's a pretty pathetic adventure board game. Kinda boring, and not very inspiring. 

Primary issue:

* A sense of reward comes solely from acquiring new items. That sense is definitely diminished once one becomes overly familiar with the range of possibilities.

* The movement, though innovative, is left lacking due to a sense of sameness about the board. Aside from access to different towns, being on one side of the map doesn't feel any different than being on the other. Yawn.

* Combat is a bit boring, especially with an audience.

* End game slumps.

* Most events/non-combat encounters feel tacked on just to break up the monotony of combat.

* No really cool special abilities between characters.

* Player interaction is minimal.

I still play it, but only because it works solitaire and it (barely) scratches my adventure board gaming itch. I suggest Arkham Horror over this mess.

RATING: 4
The concept seems like something I would like, but the game is a huge disappointment.  Under all the appealing chrome there really isn't much of a game here, and what there is takes way too long to get through.

There's ultimately one core decision to make in the game -- should I draw an easy monster for low risk and low reward, or should I draw a harder monster for high risk and high reward?  Then you roll the dice to see if you are successful or not and then repeat and repeat and repeat.

The problem is that combat, the heart of the game, is one-dimensionally dull.  All there really is to do is build up your three stats.  You might do this through equipment, allies, special abilities, or level-ups, but it's all essentially the same thing.  Get the numbers as high as possible to improve the odds of winning when you roll the die.  There's no real decisions or trade-offs to make or creative strategies to employ.

The innovative movement mechanic really doesn't amount to much.  You either get to where you want to go or you don't (and you try again next turn).  Since most items, monsters, and events are revealed randomly there really aren't many decisions to make about where you want to go -- just go towards the nearest encounter.

And there's almost no player interaction.  One player might get to a resource that another player would have liked, but that just means he'll go somewhere else instead.  You can attack each other if you want, but since combat is so dull it's again just roll the dice and see who gets to take who's item.

This lack of player interaction also means that there is very little for you to do when it's not your turn except watch and wait.  The bland world map and mindless combat means that there's not much to do even when it is your turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The game is way too dependent on dice rolling, in my opinion.  Plus, once you get too far behind, it becomes nearly impossible to catch up.  When you can't beat the green encounters, and all the green AND yellow ones are gone, you're in a very very bad place.  

Some characters are a lot better than others, which doesn't help.  

I think the game would probably be more enjoyable as a solitaire game, to be honest.  I've not tried it though, but I can see it being pretty fun.

RATING: 9.3
An adventure game I enjoy much, although it is in dire need of many official/unofficial modifications.

It is of course with some mechanics/cards from the expansions. The item costs are unbalanced. The red challenges could be more complex but the adventure variants address that issue well. It has one of the best combat systems that provides many strategic choices and interacts very good with the equipment. I also like the great illustrations and the fluff of the story (Vorakesh.. Margath.. those bastards! :D )

Since it provides a complete adventure experience I consider this the reference adventure game to which all others are measured as better or worse.

The house rules I apply:

1)progressive level up experience: First 2 levels cost 2 xp, then the cost increases by one. 2,2,3,4,5,6...

2)something to compensate for bad movement rolls:
Setup: each player receives a random ritual with cost 4 or lower from the rituals and runes expansion.
Each 3 movement dice may be spent (even after rolling) to remove one fatigue.

3)categorize the market deck to 4 decks: armor, weapons, miscelanneous and allies. The hero may choose which deck to draw from.

RATING: 6
Runebound is a more challenging and sophisticated version of Talisman: an entertaining light adventure game, full of randomness and... fun!

The game is essentially a multi-player solitaire race to character enhancement, with the ultimate goal of defeating the final boss (or three dragons). There's not much to do in other players' turns, so Runebound really works with a maximum of two or three players (otherwise the waiting times would be enormous).

For what it is, it works quite well, and it's definitely entertaining, without taxing the brain.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The second edition has improved greatly over the first edition (by leaps and bounds in several places).  I can't imagine playing the game without the extra variant rules (included officially with the base game now, and offered on the website previously when the 1st edition was available) for random encounters on the road between adventure counters, and for the doom track.  I also added in all the current small expansions that aren't new adventures, and these have added a lot of adventure variety.  (I added the expansions straightaway, since variety is the lifeblood of many an adventure game anyways, Runebound no exception.)

The market stack is now very huge, but there is a good spread of interesting items, and the items are more balanced.  You can now increase your experience in not just mind/magic/combat (which you now increase by 2 levels per counter), but also your exhaustion (2 per counter) and your life (1 per counter).  The adventures are much more challenging now; charging into the game and directly into yellow counters is a sure-fire way of spending the next few turns licking your wounds in town.  There are a lot more green adventures I would run away from, too, without at least a cheap ally and/or armor.  And fortunately, getting knocked out does leave you with your hard-earned experience points, though all your gold and your best item go away.  And nowadays, I wouldn't call Lord Margath "Margaret" without making sure he didn't hear me.

I look forwards to soon trying the adventure variants (the two small and the one big, currently) and class decks.  I most eagerly look forwards to the new slew of small expansions (hopefully more in the realm of adventure cards than market cards---the market deck is frighteningly huge right now, and I need to pare it down myself) and the big Arabian Nights-style expansion.  Fortunately, nowadays hunting Lord Margy is enough even for me.

RATING: 9.9
My favorite fantasy game.  This game is superb.  Another Wallace game that captures a light version of adventure and RPG.  The rules are easy to learn.  I would play this game anytime.  My favorite solitaire game.  

For me, the fun of Runebound was taking a weak hero and making them more powerful, using experience, items and allies. It's more about the journey than the destination, to use an old cliché. The game evokes so much theme through the flavor text, the use of weapons and allies, that I just have a blast every time I play.

I enjoy fantasy roleplaying games, and Runebound  keeps me thinking about it long after the game is over. Each game is a story:  you develop your hero in skills and weapons and then you win the game by slaying the Dragonlorand.  I am always willing to play this game. Players seeking a confrontational game in which they can constantly effect what their neighbor does may be disappointed, but those seeking an RPG experience in a board game will likely have a good time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Pleasant, Talisman-esque fantasy boardgame. Players assume a character and chart a career with the ultimate goal of foiling the forces of Darkness. The characters roam the land gaining skill, experience, and valuable prizes in fairly disconnected and inconsequential engagements (at first) and then learning more about the plans of the "villain(s)" as they choose deadlier encounters-- which is pretty cool. This may mute replay somewhat, although knowing what lies ahead matters little: the plot of the villain(s) won't progress any faster than the table wants them to. The game appears to be best with three or four players. Any more and the downtime become a serious drag, which can lead to a deflating sense of the game being little more a lather-rinse-repeat excercise. Still, I much prefer this to Talisman. RATING: 6
It's okay. I'll confess that I've only ever played this solo but my feelings have been confirmed about the game at each sitting. The mechanics represent a bulking up of a character who wanders around the wilderness fighting monsters of increasing difficulty to buy stuff to fight a big baddie at the end. I realize that I've just described many fantasy themed games, both good and bad, but with Runebound the formula is exposed in an unappealing way. This game was released before Fantasy Flight began adding more ambitious mechanics into their games; the theme needs to be the selling point. And it's not. Set in Terrinoth, we have cartoonishly depicted humanoids and monsters about which you're given a blurb. Nothing comes together thematically; I just can't be encouraged to care about any of the people, places or baddies because the it's a cursorily contrived setting which defers to mechanics, which defer back to the nonexistent story. It could be that the other small and big box expansions do something to aid this, but I think the Runebound experiment ends here for me. There are just too many other worthwhile fantasy games to explore.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The components of this game are simply wonderful! The board is nice to look at, the cards are of decent quality and the miniatures are pretty and detailed. But it's a turd dressed up as a wedding cake. Here's whats wrong: 

- It's too long: I don't mind long games, actually I really love long games. Most of my favourite games are on the long side. But all of them have their own dramaturgy. They develop into different games throughout the course of the game. With "Runebound" nothing really develops. You just get more of the same. And this doesn't keep me interested in the game for such a long time. It took us four hours completing a two player game which was essentially the same during its last rounds as it has been during its first rounds.

- It's baggy: There's a lot of text on the challenge cards, which could make for some interesting mini story lines, apart from that it doesn't. At first we read out the text loud. After the umpteenth encounter we didn't anymore, because it just didn't matter. The combat values on the card matters, but what kind of monster you are battling and why? I don't care. Gimme the gold and experience points - next player.

- It's a lot of stuff dealing with even more stuff. During the course of the game you collect stuff from the challenges, you pick up items in towns' marketplaces, and you get some allies as well. All of this adds to your abilities as a fantasy hero. But it's hard to keep track of all these modifiers and special abilities, as they are distributed among lots of different cards.

- There's no drama: Nothing really develops from the game play. There is no story, apart from the event cards, which sort of set the frame for the game. But after one play you're through with them. Even the final battles are just the same as the first battles - the challenges are just more powerful, but that's it. It's a numerical difference, not a semantical one.

- The challenges are interchangeable: After the first few you don't even read what's on the cards anymore because it's boring. The challenges don't add up to a story. They're just numbers. Nothing else. "Oh, I have to fight a coven of bloodthirsty Hospatossas who devoted their lives to the Uberbaddie? I don't care. What's their combat values again?"

I can see how this game has its fans amongst fantasy afficionados, role players and LARP fans. I'm not one of those and I won't play this overproduced sedative disguised as a boardgame  never again, not even if you threaten to kill my daughter's puppy.

RATING: 7.8
A thoroughly excellent fantasy adventure game.  This one reminds me of Return of the Heroes, but is a longer and somewhat richer experience.  Both are extremely good fantasy games.

I like the combat system in this game - andthe levelled encounters make for an altogether excellent experience.

My wife and I set this up, play an hour and walk away.  We can leave it for a day and come back with no real disruption to the game - which is excellent.

The game is simpler than the rulebook would have you believe, but a whole lot of fun.

The items, allies and experience system make this game more detailed, on top of a neat challenge, encounter and event system through which the story of the world is bled into the game experience naturally.

Great fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Runebound feels like a much more epic variation of Talisman.  While I enjoy both games, there are definitely certain aspects to Runebound that I appreciate far more.  For instance, gold doesn't grow on trees, and I often find my funds running short (as opposed to the small treasure trove my character seems to always have on hand when playing Talisman).  Battles are far more challenging, and there's something to be said for being able to push an ally in front of you to take the brunt of the damage (you say they are meant to help attack?  I say they are my meat shield!).

On the downside, the game can run a bit long.  Even playing the "fast" variant, where you level up with less experience, I feel that the game does not progress as quickly as it could.  It also suffers from older game mechanics.  Be prepared to roll them dice over, and over, and over, and...  You get the picture.

On the plus side, moving is pretty interesting.  While utilizing symbols on a die to determine which terrain you can move on may seem exhausting to some, I find it intriguing in that it helps make certain terrain types feel more difficult to traverse.

This game falls somewhere between Heroquest on the low end and World of Warcraft on the high end.  I really enjoy the ending conditions for a race game such as this (3 dragons or get lucky and kill the dragon lord) which keeps those behind in the race playing with some hope of winning.  I do however, miss the intricate character development of WoW or the long term character development of Heroquest.

RATING: 4
Tentative review based on one solo play and one two-player game.  I want this to be a good fantasy game but so far it seems very repetitive: go to encounter spot, kill monster, get treasure, wibble, wobble, wubble.  After very few turns you've seen everything this game has to offer.  The two-player game quickly saw a runaway leader emerge, and I'm not sure why that wouldn't happen with more players.  I'm not sure what the sweet spot is with the number of players, either, since there can be a lot of downtime for players.  Three seems to me to be the minimum one would want to play with.

But my biggest beef with the game is the near-complete lack of story progression.  The event cards seem to me to be intended to drive the overall story arc, but given that many events can be skipped the effect is uncertain.  What tension there is is generated by trying to keep abreast of the other players and not by any sense of expanding crisis.

RATING: 8
I like the changes in the revised edition.  The two dice normalize some of the swings of chance.  As well, the leveling up process is faster, which is good.  I like the variants as well, the timer track makes the game go much quicker.  Forces players to take more risks early rather than plodding along too slowly.  I enjoy playing this two player with my super geeky, Mur Lafferty worshipping, wife.  No downtime, and since the game is basically mult-player solitaire anyway, it's similar in play.  As with the other version, I would be very hesitant to play with more than four.  We generally play with the weaker knockout rules and quicker advancement to speed up the game.  Recommended for fantasy and RPG geeks.

RATING: 10
Two plays.  Long.  Quite fun advancing character.  Good solo game.  Three players tops, and use an easier levelling format.

Edit June 2011:  2xp/level (first eight) 3xp/level (ninth and beyond).  Three players.  Extra market card (face down) on all non-Tamalir town spaces to start game.  Wounds healed exactly as exhaustion.  No PvP garbage.  No doom track.  Way faster, same reward.  Awesome game.

Edit August 2011:  BtB solo rules without doom track.  One of my favorite games now.  Fast and furious.  About 12 plays in now.

RATING: 7
A pretty good game that plays fairly well solitaire, well with two players, and is a good game to play co-operatively (even if that was not the intention of the designer...). It does not scale well above, though. There can be a fair amount of down time if played as designed. The biggest drawback is that it takes a big time investment. An interesting observation is that (without expansions) the item deck is heavily weighted to melee and magical weapons and very light on ranged weaponry. While the expansions I had tried address this to some extent, they are still not as capable as the melee weapons that are available in the base game.

RATING: 9
Although I can understand why some don't enjoy Runebound, the interesting dice-based framework for movement and combat combined with huge variety in card decks from the expansions make it a winner in my books.

Many small choices build up, and whoever is most efficient will (most likely) be the winner. Big fan of the system overall.

My recorded play count isn't close to accurate for this game since I'm unlikely to record solo plays.

Small Box Expansions owned:
- The Cataclysm
- Champions of Kellos
- Cult of the Rune
- The Dark Forest
- The Terrors of the Tomb
- Walkers of the Wild

Big Box Expansions owned:
- The Frozen Wastes

RATING: 8
I highly recommend Runebound as a solo game.  I've played several solo rounds with some rule variants and it has been a lot of fun.  The level of challenge is perfect, it can be played in an hour to an hour and a half, and you really get a sense of progression and story as you work through.  I love building my character to fit the items available and the enemies I seem to be drawing.  No two games have been the same and often the game has come down to one dice roll.  I'll often find myself sitting down for a game of Runebound to relax; it's a lot of fun and has the same appeal you might find in a computer RPG, only you can go from start to finish in under two hours.

Once you start trying the red cards, if you fail any important rolls, it's a fast slope to losing all of your allies, weapons, and gold, and then you basically have to start over to build yourself back up again.

Hopefully some of the variants will speed it up a bit for future play-throughs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My first play left much to be desired, but now that I know what to expect (in terms of length), I may appreciate it more.  I'm still not sure if I like this or Return of the Heroes better.  Runebound definitely wins with regards to visual presentation, and that's not sayin' much.

Now that Mage Knight is out, it's gotta be mentioned as a comparison with Runebound. Both good games, but MK is really a puzzle, while RB is more of an experience game (lots of randomness). Both games are essentially a race to level up and kill the baddie. As a fan of meatier, deep gaming experiences, I definitely prefer Mage Knight. Plus, it just plays so well solo. I like the sandbox feel of MK, whereas RB seems to somewhat be a game on rails. 

Drawbacks of RB are that it's essentially the same thing over and over. Move around acquiring "stuff" to help you kill the baddies. Kill some baddies, level up, buy more stuff, and so on. It's a race game more than anything. It's certainly fun, but I can understand why some grow tired of it after a couple of hours. And the different attack styles (ranged, melee, magic) aren't really all that different beneath the surface.

RATING: 8.4
I've only played this game once so far and I'd have to say that I wish more games were like this game -- *with a little less luck*.

The thing that does strike me about this game is its ability to inject a storyline into the gameplay -- It helps build a stronger world to play in. 

YES, dice rolls and luck play a large part of this game. YES, I have pulled the 3 strength Assassin from the green deck [The toughest Green IMHO]. YES, I have been the beneficiary of a 15 cost Armor item due to card draw, but I still felt I was tromping through a game with a good leveling up system and eventually felt like I could take on any challenge due to the decisions I made. [Although one of my last decisions ended up getting me baked to a crisp by Margoth :( -- but mostly because I only had 1 ally at the time! Silly me.]

Although I am a huge fan of little to no-luck strategy games, I think the storyline and atmosphere this game builds on is something many games in which you 'roll dice and draw cards' lack.

For people who play games to consistenly outwit and beat their opponents handily, this is not the best game to have consistent outcomes [then again what game with dice and cards usually is?] But if you're looking for a more involved game [time wise] with more atmosphere and an engaging world, Runebound has that in abundance.

There will certainly be those who feel after playing a game for three hours and NOT winning would be a waste of time - [especially when one or two dice rolls rather than strategic placement may have determined the outcome], I, on the other hand, felt it a compelling game that I would like to try with perhaps the weakest character in the deck -- just for the challenge alone.

I classify it as a Light Medium weight game because of the 'fiddley' rules.  Not a lot of strategy involved, but very fun. RATING: 5
Very colorful, with nice graphics and cool flavor.  Of course, it suffers from the limitations of the RPG-Boardgame genre -- at heart, it's a dice-rolling exercise with only minimal decision-making.  If you like that sort of game (Talisman, Heroquest, Return of the Heroes, etc.), then you'll almost certainly enjoy this.  If you don't dig the genre, then stay away unless you're really in the mood for swords 'n' sorcery.

The game also has a serious downtime issue, with very little player interaction -- with 4 players, we passed the movement dice to the next player so he could roll and plan his move while the current player resolved his turn.  Otherwise, it would have become very dull very quickly.  I imagine that with any more than 4 players, it would really be excruciating.

RATING: 8

Personally, I would prefer an action point system for movement, but ymmv. None of this keeps the game from being a blast to play and I would recommend it to fantasy gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great game when you're in the mood for lots of theme and lots of chance! Dice chucking fun, but with more than three players, way too long.

We now play with the following movement variant to speed up the game:

Players get 6 movement points (MP) on each turn, subtracting 1 for each wound above 2.
* Cities/Roads/Plains cost 1MP (max 4)
* Hills/River/Forest cost 2MP
* Mountains/Swamps cost 3MP
Cards that grant movement dice rerolls give an extra MP instead. Players may spent N+1MP to regain N exhaustion.

We also play with a variant that divides the market deck into separate stacks for armor, weapons, magic items, and allies. During the market step, a d10 is rolled to see which type is added to the city deck. Players can also choose one type, draw 3 cards, and may purchase as many as they can afford.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The second edition vastly improved the game balance and progression.  Now gold is in short supply and you need to be sure you are strong enough not only to win challenges but to take minimal damage doing so.  Talisman will always have a place in my heart, but this is now my favorite RPG-type boardgame.  I particularly like the 4 different encounter decks that allow you to take on greater challenges as you level up your character.  This is my favorite epic dungeon crawl type game.

UPDATE June 2016
I finally revisited this game after many years.  I played 3rd edition a little while back, and really enjoyed it, but it was a different game in many ways, so I did not think it would replace 2nd edition for me.  I like the way 2nd edition lets you chose the difficulty of your encounters and this is a huge leap over Talisman and the like.  However, I fear my gaming tastes have evolved over time, and I no longer get much thrill out of the move, draw a card, and roll dice sequence, no matter how thematic.  Firefly the Game failed for me not too long ago for similar reasons.  You roll high you win, you roll low you lose,and that's about it.  All the variety boils down to a different picture over the number you need to roll.  I have grown to want more, and games have grown to give me more.  

My shelves now contain Eldritch Horror, Runebound 3rd, and my beloved Assault on Doomrock, all of which offer a more interesting game experience and challenge resolution mechanism.  Original Arkham Horror is a contemporary to Runebound 2nd, but has held up better, I think, thanks to its more interesting decision space, even in the face of its epic fiddliness.  Runebound 2nd will always have a warm place in my heart, but I don't know if there is space for it on my shelves any more.

RATING: 7.5
At first I wasn't sold on this one. It felt random and fiddly. Draw a card, read the text, make some random check for this or that, procede. But as our game went on and the number of items, allies and undefeated challenges mounted, I started to see where this game shines. 

There are indeed tough and relevant decisions to be made, and making the right choices can be a challenging and rewarding experience. I found the overall story plot to be a bit cheesy and unoriginal, but overall the flavor text and artwork combine admirably to convey the theme. I soon found myself having fun, almost against my will. And gaining that next level always seems just a turn or two away, so I found that I kept wanted to push onward, onward... evidence of a good design. 

The game is not without its flaws. As others have pointed out, it can go on too long, and it's basically a multi-player solitaire excercise. No, it's not perfect, but it's still better than I was expecting. And I like it more than Dungeoneer.

RATING: 6
Okay, I hate to drag out the most over-used and abused phrase on BGG, but here it is:

Multiplayer Solitare

It's not a cooperative game at all. My game group had two tables of four players each playing Runebound Second Edition, and there was so much downtime for individual players that we'd walk over to the other table, and chat about how their game was going. One table finished after 3.5 hours, the other called the game at 4 hours. 

The theme seemed nice, but as a player I resented the time/money sinks placed into the game. If you died a lot early, you were pretty much behind the rest of the game. We were playing with cheaper skills and "soft knockouts", and it still took too long. The movement dice are the overly frustrating tools of the devil.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Good.  See?  Ameritrash games can be multiplayer solitaire too!

There's some good stuff though:  Awesomely dark feel, hilariously corny flavor text, but oh-so-long.

I'm beginning to think that the "D&D as a boardgame" is as ephemeral as the "Civ Lite boardgame" -- in both cases, an integral part of the experience is that fact that you invest a huge amount of time into your character/civilization.  Trying to pare it down to 90 minutes or so also removes the emotional buy-in that makes it click.  Runebound takes forever, but due to the downtime in a multi-player game, you don't actually invest all that much individual time in your own character.

RATING: 8
Runbound 2nd edition is quite different than 1st edition.  The game is more difficult for the players, and I have been seeing quite a bit more 'knockouts' than in 1st edition.  Equipment is still king and it's still very much a fanstasy adventure game.  Regarding expansions: The market deck expansions add so much to the deck, that I have been considering a house rule of drawing 1 additional market card... otherwise there is the tendancy to not draw enough xxxx.  The amount of additional heroes is such that we draw 3 to start and pick one; then everyone reveals their hero at the same time.  We have added the adventure cards to the deck and play as 'normal'.  The character deck expansions add a lot of potential interaction; but so far it's been that... potential.  Not many abilities are used to allow the potential, in our experience.  Regardless, Runebound is a very enjoyable game.  The character decks add *much* to the player interaction.  Otherwise, it's pretty much a solo race to the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I got into the whole Runebound thing early on in my re-entry into the hobby in 2008 and fell in love with it. It brought Talisman back to life in a much more interesting way, and with a bajillion expansions, it seemed like a great system to invest in, which I did. After enjoying many plays, I eventually sold my collection off somewhat regretfully in order to raise funds for some unexpected bills, but in truth, my playing had dropped off considerably. Still, I think it's a great choice for some.

The game delivers as an immersive adventure that picks up where Talisman left off by taking you on a ride from Average Joe to Junior Badass to Total Badass. The combat system is interesting and dangerous and forces you to balance your character's stats. You collect items that confer all kinds of crazy offensive and defensive benefits. The fun is going on the ride from picking your fights carefully to knowing you're a wrecking crew with the coolest stuff that fears nothing.

As others will tell you, the game is best for 1-3 people. I preferred it with 2P and with house rules. In fact, one of the strengths of the game is that it totally lends itself to variants and house rules. I preferred rules that made people level up more evenly and only able to purchase items 2x their current level. Basically, I used a modified variant of Sonnenblume's rules changes that you can find in the file section. Depending on how random you want the game to be or not, I guarantee you can house rule it to your satisfaction, and I highly doubt that any two people play RB the exact same way. 

I said above that my playing had dropped off and that was because I lost my primary RB partner, and just as I was organizing things in preparation to re-enter the RB world (possibly solo), I discovered Mage Knight which is a different type of adventure altogether, but one that has got my full attention. When some bills came up, I decided to sell my RB stuff as a lot of it was OOP, but I'd still happily sit and play the game given the chance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
With its shallow and repetitive gameplay, Runebound understandably turns off some people. To me it's the board game equivalent of the old NES Dragon Warrior games that I loved as a kid. Grind. Kill. Tool up. Kill. Die. Tool up. Then return to that failed encounter for sweet, sweet mindless revenge. With that mindset, it's an always enjoyable adventure game, and the race aspect adds needed tension. My biggest complaint is the not with the simple mechanics but with the flavor of the base game. It has an uninspired genre fantasy coat of paint that I dislike. I much prefer the slightly darker Terrinoth of Descent or DungeonQuest or the "traditional," more literary fantasy theme of Magic Realm. Best as a two-player game since PvP can slow the experience to a crawl while producing a runaway leader; however, without PvP you are playing parallel games on the same board.

RATING: 8
My #2 choice for fantasy adventure boardgames. 

I really like Runebound, except it goes on a bit long at times. If you're looking for a more combat-oriented adventure game, then choose Runebound. It works best with two, and I wouldn't even think of adding more than 3, as the game would drag on for far too long if you have kids or time is tight. It works very well as a two-player game, and doesn't tend to last more than a couple of hours with just the Lady and I.

Little player interaction, and little ability to affect the other player's strategy might make this one fall a bit flat for those with a very competitive nature. This can be remedied with the character decks, but they also add even more time to an already lengthy game. 

The Lady and I enjoy it quite a bit, and it sees a lot of time off the shelf. The huge number of expansions, and sheer number of cards make this one an excellent choice if you're seeking a lot of variety in your adventure game.

RATING: 8
Enjoyable, but I won't play again with more than two players - it really drags, and there's nothing to plan during your opponents' turns.  I actually like Runebound best as a solitaire game, which takes about an hour using the guide posted by MrSkeletor (threat difficulty of 14 or 15 makes for a nice challenge).  Another reason to play Runebound solo is that Prophecy is a better multiplayer game by leaps and bounds, but doesn't play especially well as a solitaire game.  

I love Runebounds's combat system and I also like the movement system.  However, the board play is not as good as it should be.  The board's geography isn't very meaningful because the encounters aren't revealed until you visit them.  The cities suffer from the same problem: very few of the items available are known before you actually visit the location.  And since the items and allies are what will determine whether you win the game, the extreme randomness can be annoying.

RATING: 7
This is a good game.  It's a light adventure game where the player controls a party of 1 to 3 characters (1 Hero and up to 2 Allies).

The combat is pretty straight forward, as players add 2d10 with their ability rating, and compare that number with the creatures rating.  This may easily become monotonous.

The market mechanic is interesting, as players visit towns where they may purchase items, hire allies, pay to recoup stamina and/or health, not to mention sell items (for half price).

The players control which ability of his/her Hero will be upgraded when he/she reaches the next experience level (required XP varies based on number of players).

The XP provided by creatures varies depending on the deck it came from: Green = 1, Yellow = 2, Blue = 3 and Red = 4.

The game may appear to be long at first when you are learning to play, but it quickly becomes intuitive.  Even Player versus Player combat is pretty easy.

Adding expansions will be rather straight forward, so there is no need to worry about replayability.  Creative players could easily design their own adventures, creatures and items!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
[Initial Impression: 2; Unlikely to play again]
I was excited to be playing Runebound - it's by Martin Wallace, a designer I regard highly, has an appealing theme, is very well produced, and I'd heard good things about it.  I came away more disappointed than I think I've ever been by any game. In short, there are almost no meaningful decisions, the theme wears off quickly, and the game just wears on and on.

First, the good: Runebound is dripping with theme, and players will enjoy reading their cards, describing the events they encounter and the monsters they are fighting, and there are some tense moments when you're not sure whether you'll make it out of a particular scrape.  The game is very well produced, with colorful and appealing bits (although the hero minis would look a lot cooler if they were painted).

That's it for the good, now the bad: If ever there was a dice-fest, this is one (and I'm generally okay with some random elements in a game, as long as you can meaningfully affect or react to that randomness).  Dice control movement, by showing the terrain types you can travel over.  Dice determine the effects of most events and the outcome of every battle.  Because you gain upgrades, the dice rolls needed to beat the harder baddies are about the same as the necessary dice rolls for the earlier battles, so there's not much feeling of progress or of escalating tension.  There is almost no player interaction - you just happen to be moving your heroes around on the same board, and another hero might get around to buying an item you wanted first.  It is possible to fight one another, but players progress at a similar pace, so it would be generally unadvisable to risk your spoils to pick on someone else.

Finally, the ugly (and it's quite ugly):  The problem with the dice controlling everything is that it reduces the meaningful decisions to almost zero.  The only real decisions (and even these have fairly straight-forward choices) are: 1) what to buy (although there are some obviously superior items), 2) when to start attempting harder challenges (when you're breezing through the current level adventures), and 3) what to upgrade (get enough on each ability to protect yourself, and then concentrate on your most damaging attack).  The other "decisions" are what dice-limited route can I take to get to an adventure or city on this turn, and when (if ever) do I try to escape an especially powerful monster?  The movement dice almost break the game.  I once spent 4 turns not moving anywhere meaningful because the dice didn't show the terrain types I needed. It might be possible to improve this aspect by assigning movement points, and have each terrain type require a different number of points to enter.  As for the battle system, I felt like you were just hoping for the right number or higher to come up on each dice roll - you could play essentially the same game with a deck of cards and a pair of dice: flip a card, and that's the number you have to beat with your dice roll.  I will say that the game is initially fun, for about 30-45 minutes, as you read off the cards and get immersed in the theme.  After that, however, I realized that the theme was just a veneer for the dice engine, and even the other players (3 of whom did say they enjoyed the game - their reasons, incidentally, were "you don't have to think too much", and "I like that we can talk during other players' turns") stopped reading all the thematic card text and just started saying things like, "I need an 11 to defend, and then an 8 to make the kill".  And that's how the next 4+ hours progressed (the full game took just over 5 hours).  I was bored, bored, bored.  After all the players finally worked up to the hardest (red) challenge level, the third red challenge was the game ending Lord High Whatever, which provided a brief moment of tense battle - the hero was relatively weak going in, but was able to use some items and got some lucky dice rolls to win fairly easily.  Even she said she didn't feel like she deserved to win (one of the other players had a much stronger and well-armed hero), so even the endgame felt randomly determined.  This game might be okay for an hour or so, but it is an insanely long and boring slog (and we were playing the "short" version).  I read the "Doom Track" and "Hazard" variants, and both seem like they might help the game.  The "Doom Track" seems especially essential, as it adds a variable turn limit - and anything that would shorten the game can only be good.  Finally, the feeling of low player interaction is overwhelming.  You sometimes have to wait quite awhile for your next turn, watching others roll dice and announce their results, and I felt it just didn't matter what their result was - it wouldn't change my actions in any way.  We were passing the movement dice ahead so that each player could plan their movement in advance, and I think the game would play just as well if everyone had their own set of dice and just rolled simultaneously and announced their actions.  The rules say you can play with one and I think that may be the best number to use.

I never thought a Wallace game would be among my lowest-rated, but this is the kind of game experience I would pay money to avoid.  This rating is admittedly based on one play, but sometimes that's all you need to make a valid judgment - and it's highly likely that I'll never have a second play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great game with exceptional support and development from FFG. I would not recommend more than 3 players and in my mind it has a sweat spot of 2 players, and using the Doom Track is very worthwhile. If you like role-playing, talisman or just fantasy in general you cannot go wrong with this game. The cards are very much like a CCG and offer the main core mechanic and variety to the game system.

I have made some home rules including improving the money, loss after death etc. One of the main changes is the way I use market decks as shown in the Martin Knight variant.

When a player visits a town he must choose between the 5 Markets (Weapon Smith, The Armourer, The Tavern (Allies), The Enchanter Store (Relics/Artefacts), The Market Place (for cards that are drawn during the standard market step). This gives the game a huge amount of flavour, keeps the items flowing and also incentive for players to travel from one town to the next.

Also take a look at the file QuickRunebound.pdf... excellent suggestions to improve the game.

Un bon jeu d'affrontement si vous aimez la Fantasy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Adventure! There's a lot to like about this game. You go on encounters, get some fat lute, get xp, and kill more monsters. It's like a video game on its own. So far, I'm liking everything about this game. The components are great, although the cards could use better art. Yeah, I'm an art snob.

The main problem with the game is that it's basically a single player game. The amount of time waiting for your turn is atrocious. Roll, decide what you want to do, encounter, decide how you want to face it... it's way too much to wait. 

If you're playing this as a single player game, you'll have fun (I did), but when I introduced it to the group, it came across as a bit stale. The only real interaction comes in with the PVP, and although that's great, the only real reason to do it is if you can win against your opponent. It's a unique feature at least.

Runebound could have been a great game, if it were not for just a single problem: The fact that the developers forgot that it was a board game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I start out enjoying this game, but always end up feeling frustrated. The game is way too long, but the Doom Track fix forces an artificial and anti-climactic endgame. The movement dice concept seems like a cool idea in theory, but in practice tends to bite mightily.

My initial rating was a '9' but it's steadily decreased since then.

Problems include (but are not limited to): crushing downtime, trivial choices, multi-player solitaire, telescoping playing time lengthens dramatically with each additional player even when using the Doom Track, requires house rules out of the box to make it playable, but the number one negative in my view is...

Dice rule all. If your character is tough but you roll lots of 1s and 2s, even wimpy green adventures will knock you out. On the other hand, if you consistently roll 9s and 10s, even a first level character will beat red adventures! The game is entirely about rolling dice, and so has more in common with Craps or Yahtzee than with fantasy roleplaying.

This could have been a great game with lots of theme flavor. Instead, mighty battles are reduced to "I need to roll an 8 or higher here."

RATING: 6
No comment made on initial play. I recall that it was a rather long and drawn-out affair, but the final battle was exciting. In that regard, it reminds me of Arkham Horror, but this was way better.

Update (10/01/11): After meditating on this for several years, and having played Talisman, Prophecy, Dungeon Quest, Duel of Ages, Arkham Horror, etc., I can appreciate what this design is trying to do. The scale is more worldly, as I feel like I am wandering about a large fantasy-world, going from encounter to encounter. Talisman is a roll-and-move in the purest sense, and thus feels like Monopoly, although you can go in either direction. Prophecy feels like a hybrid between Talisman and Runebound--the "world" is a circular track, although the movement is deterministic (no dice). I may be talked into revisiting this once again.

RATING: 10
[center][size=6][COLOR=#878787]LIFETIME GAME [microbadge=28495]:star:[microbadge=28496] "PLAY FOREVER"[/COLOR][/size][/center]

NOTE:  Rating is for the [i]Runebound[/i] system as a whole—the base game, with nothing but the "Rise of the Dragonlords" adventure, pulls a solid 8.  It soars to a 10 when considered with the plethora of expansions, especially the Adventure Variants (each with a different overarching plot).

A fun adventure game system from the "pull a card, read the encounter, roll some dice" family.  Can be boring with dull people—it requires a certain amount of storytelling ability to make it interesting:

Q:  [i]Why do you get a plus to Diplomacy after you kill the Hill Troll?[/i]

A:  [i]Because you made a backpack out of his scrotum, that's why.[/i]  

Be sure to read all the flavor text aloud—dramatically—for best effect.  (This isn't really optional; the plot is gradually revealed through the flavor text as the players progress through the adventure decks.  On balance it's less "flavor text" than it is "story".)

Plays best with 2, though I might be persuaded to play with 3 if everyone is knowledgeable and peppy and takes their turns with preternatural alacrity.  The claim that it plays with 6 is a cruel joke that we fell for exactly once, never to be repeated again—it ate [i]an entire day[/i] to no good effect.

UPDATE:  We managed 9 plays of 1st Edition, and that was without expansions.  As this is a "Saturday afternoon" game (taking several hours) that means we got somewhere around two months' worth of Saturdays out of it.  Two months of solid play off a single scenario.  With all the scenarios FFG pumped out for this—12 total—the replay value blows through the roof, soars into orbit and then takes a hard left into a magical wormhole.  HUGE amount of bang-for-buck!

UP-UPDATE:  Tweaks that make for more gaming goodness:

1)  Use the "shorter game" rules, making it easier to level up.

2)  Use "softer knockouts" to prevent table flippage.*

3)  Separate the massive market deck into three piles:  the Tavern (allies), the Smithy (weapons & armor), and the Bazaar (a catch-all for familiars, magic items & artifacts).  This allows players to really target the holes in their kit/entourage without endless turns of market-stepping loads of junk they don't need.

4)  Ignore PvP.  We found it just slowed things down and lead to hard feelings.**

[size=7]*After too many rounds of bad rolls and bad beats I [i]finally[/i] manage to scrape together enough gold to buy the magic sword that dovetails neatly with my special abilities—only to get my ass kicked by a wild boar and lose the sword.

**After too many rounds of bad rolls and bad beats I [i]finally[/i] manage to scrape together enough gold to buy the magic sword that dovetails neatly with my special abilities—only to get my ass kicked by you and lose the sword.[/size]

While these tweaks speed up the game, I'm not necessarily saying the game [i]needs[/i] speeding up.  For a lazy Saturday afternoon (especially a bad weather one) this game expands to fill the time nicely.  We've found that the above tweaks will take an afternoon game and turn it into a evening game—and that just means we get to play more!

UP-UP-UPDATE:  [b]10 June 2015[/b]—Completed my set with an unopened, unpunched, sun-bleached [i]Mists of Zanaga[/i] that had been kicking around an FLGS for God knows how long... (Well, for at least five years, right?)

RATING: 8.5
(Second Edition) After disliking the first edition, my friend and I rewrote the rulebook.  We came bizarrely close the the 2nd Edition rules.  I am really warming to the game, and will be purchasing some expansions to give it more legs. 

Update:  After playing some of the expansions, this game continues to be a good time.  I think you have to play to "enjoy the ride" rather than be concerned about balance issues, etc.  Go buy stuff and kill stuff.  

Another update:  I have taken out a few cards from the deck which I consider to be "less than fun", and the game is really starting to hum.  It's a terrific solo play as well, using the Threat rules from Midnight.

Yet Another Update:  I typically only play this solo and 2-player now, and I have developed an excellent variant using the character decks as an "AI".  It's getting played a lot, and I still want to collect the expansions, so that's a good thing.

RATING: 6
Variants I'm trying:

***Starting Gear:  A card worth 1 or 2 gold & 3 or two gold, repectively.

***Players always receive 5 movement dice (even when wounded).

***Faster healing: 2 gold in town heals all wounds & fatigue on hero.  To heal an Ally costs additional 2 gold per Ally.  1 gold heals 1 wound & all fatigue.

Then, when outside a town, can allocate movement dice to try & heal before moving.  Each die symbol that matches terrain heals 1 wound from hero or Ally.  The remainder of dice are used for movement.

***Can always move 1 space even if movement dice don't match.  (But can't move if don't roll ANY dice to move - e.g. heal.)

***Recover Fatigue after rolling:  You may choose to rest after rolling the movement dice. Roll all the movement dice you have available and then, if you choose not to use any of them, remove fatigue at a 1 per die basis.

***Toughness: need wounds to exceed life to KO hero or kill monster.

***Travel Hazard:  If end turn in a empty, non-town space roll 2 movement dice.  If neither matches the terrain you are on: Night Hazard.  If either matches then travelling salesman: draw one card from market deck & may purchase! 

Night Hazard: Roll D10 - 1-2 Green, 3-8 Yellow, 9-10 Blue.  No before combat, can't escape on first round, no reward, do get experience.

***KO:  Lose next turn - just stand character up with wounds = health.  The turn after they can start trying to heal, etc.

***Experience: XP needed = next level.  Start level = 0 so need 1 for first advancement.

***Market Step: Each turn each player will take a market step, regardless of whether the character is in a town. At the beginning of the game, place a token next to the Market stack for Frostgate. During the Market step, if the character is in town, the Market step takes place as usual. If the character is not in town, advance the token to the next town’s Market stack (moving down the board, then back to Frostgate), then draw a Market card and place the card in the Market stack indicated by the token. So, every turn, one town will receive a new item for purchase.  In addition, to simulate the volatility of the marketplace, each town’s market will only carry one of each type of good. One Ally, one Armor, one magic (Artifact or Rune or Ritual), and one Weapon. Basically, the first word on the card in the bold print identifies its type. If an Item/ Ally card is drawn of a type which already has a type available in that town’s marketplace, the new card replaces the old, and the old is discarded back to the deck. This may cause an item to be removed from the shop when a character is in a town during a Market phase! This can be a real disappointment when a character travels across the map to hire an ally, only to find he's left town that very day!

***Equipment limits: Unchanged - two weapons, 1 armour, 2 allies.



Boardless variant I'm keen to try some time: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1075181/card-game-variant-for-runebound



Another market variant:
[q="nelud"]1. When a hero enters a city at the beginning of market step he reveals cards one by one until their total printed cost exceeds his money (instead of drawing single card). He sets aside these cards, but treats them as though they are in the city market.

2. When he finishes his market step, he must place in the city market one most expensive card of those set aside (that remains not purchased). He discards others of those set aside cards (if any).[/q]

RATING: 8
Runebound is a good game but heavily mired by its absolutely ridiculous length.  This game is way, way, WAY too long for what it is.  Other than that, there's a nice feel of character evolution and more than enough theme to drown in.  However, the constant dice chunking can get old, the movement dice idea is pretty stupid (what was wrong with action points?), dying can be a huge set back, interaction is lacking, and the downtime is annoying.  Despite all of that, I still think it's not bad and wouldn't mind playing again if there were some way to really speed it up.

Update:  This is my average rating.  Solo with a threat track, I give Runebound a 10 (mainly for the ability to "pause" whenever you want).  As a co-op game with two, I give it a 9.  The game as written with two to three players is a 7.  If any more players are added past three, my rating pretty much drops a point each time.  I still want to know how Martin Wallace designed this.  You mean the Martin Wallace who created Age of Steam, Byzantium, and Tempus?  That Martin Wallace?!

RATING: 6.5
High fantasy fun with easy rules and plenty of expansions for infinite variation.  A perfect "Gateway" game for those who love fantasy theme but are uninitiated in the ways of designer boardgames such as "Descent: Journeys in the Dark" or "Dungeon Lords".  While not much strategy is involved, and player interaction is limited, there is still fun to be had here, epically for inexperienced gamers who are interested in trying something beyond monopoly.
Runebound was my first game from Fantasy Flight Games and my introduction into the wonderful world of Ameritrash style games.  As such, it holds a special place in my heart and still sees the occasional return to the gaming table.

RATING: 8
Seems pretty much the same as the 1st edition with some minor changes.  Was it really worth all the aggravation of reprinting? I love the game except for the leveling up at the beginning of the game. Why does it cost as much and therefore MUCH easier to level up late in the game???  In every fantasy game I've ever played it was much EASIER to level up quickly early on and it required more and more experience to level up in the higher levels.  House rules are MUCH needed regarding this, it's really what drags the game out so long. 

Well, that and the movement dice. There had to be a better way, it just takes too long to explore all the options of your movement dice, especially the AP prone. I bought an extra set of movement dice just so the next player can roll their dice and start thinking about their turn too.   

Stick to three players, four at the max, otherwise the game drags very slowly and there is a ton of downtime between players.

RATING: 4
This game includes several of my least favorite mechanics in one really brightly designed and attractive package.  Played as designed without any variant or rule changes, those are:  

1. Lots of downtime:  Turns can be long and the more players you have, the worse this gets.

2. Omnipotence:  Chance across a certain combination of items and you become nearly invulnerable in combat.  I've seen this happen in nearly every game I've been involved in, such that usually someone ends up running away with the game. 

3. The Permascrewed "Oops" factor:  I've found this game to be very unforgiving of mistakes or deaths, generally, in that once you fall sufficiently behind (or even behind at all, on occasion) it's game over, and you end up sitting through round after round, plus downtime, when you have no chance to win or possibly even compete anyhow.

#3 isn't bad by itself (one of the better games I've played, Power Grid, shares this), but in combination with the other "flaws" and longer game length this isn't a game I enjoy playing very often.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A decent game of its type; nice for solo play (even though the box says 2-6 players). (In)famously, the game often runs much longer than advertised on the bok; extra movement dice and/or rolling movement while another player is playing are probably good ways to address this. The Doom Track is a poor way to speed up the game, and it's well-nigh impossible to use solo; the Threat Track (available in the Midnight expansion, described on FFG's Web site, and re-presented on BGG) is a superior alternative that can help keep the pressure on. Player interaction can be very low in this game; class decks may help here (I don't have them). Expansions generally enrich this game, although it's developing expansionitis for me, with 18 small decks and 2 large expansions available now or soon (as of 8/06) and more on the way.

12/28/07: Traded this away, along with the 10 small-deck expansions I had.

RATING: 6
A top-view RPG game where players move around a world map, rushing to level up in a race to beat the red quests. There is an interesting movement system attached to the game, which attempts to simulate adventurers getting lost in the world. The game is pretty simple to pick up since there aren't too many choices for the players each turn. Quests have been streamlined into 2 basic types: Skill rolls and Combat; and for this reason, the game seems to get monotonous pretty fast.

Still, this is a pretty good RPG for 1 or 2 players. Note that each player plays a solitaire game, so my BGG rating would drop 1 point for each additional player.

RATING: 3
From my experience this game plays best solo. Less players is definitely better.  The main reason is that this game has an almost criminal amount of downtime where there is truly nothing to do. If you prefer a lighter, let's-use-a-game-to-force-us-to-sit-around-and-talk game this might be useful in ways other, more weighty games, aren't. This is paid for, however, by a v e r y   l o n g   gaming experience, almost rivaling some much weightier wargames I've played.

The randomness of drawing and dice can create very divergent player strengths; even after only a few rounds some players will be having a lot of fun, having very strong and acquiring wonderful things, while other players will be miserable, having been killed by a green slime or something and will be wandering around getting pummeled and desperately hoping for a weak enemy to acquire experience.

RATING: 6
Nice fantasy game, strong on theme. But there are many things that are really disappointing - I miss a randomly generated board and a more intersting character development. Just choosing between three stats is not that thrilling... And the fixed board makes the game a bit boring when you played it many times one after the other. And first of all: WAY TOO MUCH DOWNTIME. Games tend to last for four to six hours in which you most time sit around waiting for other players to end their turn... The Al´Kalim-Expansion too is nice done - amazing look, but too hard (15 as starting rolls in comparison to 12 or 13 in the original runebound...). And even much more dice rolling in comparison to the standard runebound: story-roll, night ambushes, calculating exhaustion from day-travel... that slows the game down even more... :( In total Runebound has potencial for higher ratings, but the massive downtime and the very luck based character development cost many points...

During the mid to late game, you start wasting turns just walking across the map because it starts to become empty of things to do.  It starts to feel like the board is completely unnecessary.  

This is why Talisman is superior, because the map becomes more interesting, not less, over the course of the game.  Still a very fun game though and it gets played often because of the "zero to hero" feel the game gives you.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Is you like RPGs you will like Runebound. You get a good sense of adventure. Most of that comes from the great card artwork. The rules are simple, and the great rulebook makes the game is easy to learn. The game is customizable and a lot of special rules and variants exist.Expansion should expand on and already good game. Downtime with 2 player is minimal. lasts about 2-3 hours( plus 1h for each additional player). Although good, I was expecting more in the actual map quality. (The map looks pixalized). And I would add maybe more choice in character customization.

The game takes a little too long a 3 and more players. 
I Recommend playing with these variant:

1. The games starts too slow: To fix this, players could start with a lot more gold (maybe 5 ) or start with a 1 gold item or ally.

2. Encounters are rare, especially near the end game: todo play with random encounter variant

3. For shorter play: each level should take 3 experience.

4.Choice at the market is too limiting: Fix is to draw 2 cards at the market step and choose one to put in the market.

To test:
AutoHeal when gaining level? To speed up game play early on.

Find A less dangerous KO rule. Instead of lossing gold and highest item, the player looses one turn?

RATING: 10
I feel like I shouldn't like this game as much as I do. It's slow and if you play it with other players, you rarely interact with them.

But it has a lot of charm, scratches that high-fantasy and character leveling itch, and it is just plain fun. I love all the items, allies, monsters, heroes. The components are top notch, combat can be fun/challenging, you can play solo and it is highly expandable. 

There is just too much here to enjoy that it manages to overcome any complaints I mentioned. Not to mention, it is strangely addicting. But as with most games, it is not for everyone.

Playing with two (or maybe three) is the way to go for sure. With two it's a great time, with 4+ it is just way too much downtime.

RATING: 9
This game is a must have.  If you are old school and have played games like Talisman or Dark Tower then you will be thrilled with this game.  Basically take elements of a RPG, questing, gathering treasure, improving your character and roll them up in to a board game.  I highly recommend playing with most of the (recommended) optional rules and maybe making a few house rules to fit your style of play.  Runebound is PVP enabled, but I have only felt the need to use it once, and the many times I have played we have only had one clear cut winner, which is good.  Like I said this is a must have game if you are into fantasy adventure, you will not regret it.  And they just released a bunch of new content that you can pick up in expansion packs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fun little game.  A bit more complex than Talisman, and a bit less luck-driven than Talisman (which I like), although there is still quite a bit of luck.

I originally played without any doom \ threat\ timer of any sort.  I found that the game took way too long and it lacked tension.  We got way too powerful and easily beat the final challenges.

I put the game aside for about a year and recently got back into it again.  This time I tried the doom \ threat track and I found the game much better, and a lot more exciting!

I highly recommend playing with either doom or threat track variant.  I also recommend buying a few of the small card expansions to give the game more variety, otherwise you'll soon get bored with the core set.  Unfortunately, it is virtually impossible to find most of the card expansions these days, at least for reasonable prices.

If you like Talisman, and light RPG games, I highly recommend this game.  It is fun, and pretty simple.

RATING: 6
Generally, I don't like character-building battle games, as I feel the strategy is way too straightforward and the luck of the dice comes into play more often than not.  While Runebound definitely suffers from those characteristics, I still find myself enjoying it.  It's not the greatest game in the world and doesn't really distinguish itself from other battle games, but I feel like there's enough world exploration and entertaining gameplay to keep me satisfied for the length of the game.  It definitely suffers from a lack of catch-up mechanics (especially in the two-player game), as an early battle loss can prevent you from catching up to your unbeaten opponents.  The entertainment value will probably fade pretty quickly for me, but for now, I enjoy this game.

RATING: 2
Much too long for what it is - you have similar battles, over and over and over. For me, it ended up feeling like an MMO, grinding for levels.

The system looks like it could have potential. But this basic set just lacks. You have a nice map - but it doesn't matter, everywhere is the same. I kind of expect some sort of magic or special places on a fantasy map. The characters are uninspired, possibly because fitting them onto a card the same size as all the others seemed to have priority over making them interesting. Character development is meh, your stats go up, yea... The story line... isn't. There's signs of what could be there in the form of event and encounter cards that are mixed into the encounter decks, but they are too few IMO. There were a couple of encounters that, story-line-wise, involved actually helping out someone by travelling to some place on the map, and they were a very nice break from the 98% of them that were no more than "kill this monster".

Maybe the expansion games are better developed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A good addition to the Fantasy Game Genre but requires lots of tweaks to keep it from growing long and dull. I like it best with 2 people and wouldn't really play with 3 or 4.  It works solo which is ok.  Epic, grand adventures, not really a 'play to win' type of game but an experience.  Wonderful expansions.  Lots of options/cards to tailor the game to your liking and lots of good variants posted around here to help you streamline play.  The threat track is a must to keep the game fresh.  I like to play with 3 market stacks (1-4; 5-9; 10+; use a 1d3 to determine a stack for a random draw)and 3 starting cards in each market (blind) with a market draw from the players choice of stacks (depending on what they can afford).  In addition to that I like the starting equipment variant and the three points to advance variant.  Those are just a couple of things you can do to really help speed the game along and keep the playing time reasonable. Lastly, this should really be played as a co-op game, playing this game competitively feels like a real expense of time.  

Ultimately, I prefer Citadel of Blood, DeathMaze, Prophecy, Return of the Heroes, or Magic Realm.

Expansions all sold.
Base game sold.

RATING: 10
This is a cracker of a Fantasy Adventure Game. Sure it is all about leveling up, acquiring the right item combos and the like but it is balanced so well with its 4 levels of adventure decks that help the action scale along with you.

As a multi-player it can really drag so I wouldn't recommend playing more than the occasional game with 3+ but with 2 it is great.

Where it really shines though is in the solo play (using Mr. Skeletor's solo variant - see BGG forums for details) that it offers. I can crank these at in about 90 minutes each and they remind me of plying Fighting Fantasy Gamebooks as a kid.

The only warning here is that you will see the cards in the Adventure decks within 5-7 plays so a regular supply of Deck Expansions is a must to mix up the action.

The big box expansions also throw new elements into the mix. 

I'm loving this game and am so glad I found it.

RATING: 6
Good. Runebound is the closest experience to RPG Dungeons and Dragon inside a Board Game box.  The game plays almost as a solitary game with two players, even if you enjoy how the other player defeats his enemies. The interaction is almost null within the game, and if you star adding more players to the game, you will suffer a penalty of game time... yep, almost 4 hours a two player game... with more, you can reach 8 or even more hours as I've been told.
The doom counter rules are mandatory to put a clock on the game.

Nice Dungeons and Dragons for two players though.

2007/03/01: One of the biggest issue about this game, is that when a player takes the lead, it's very hard to catch for the remaining players (I've only played this game with two other players).

2007/11/11: The leading player issue is solved with character decks.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I gave the first edition a 4 and this edition a 6.  As a competitive PvP game, I'd still rate this as a 4.  However, I play the second edition as a co-op game with my two daughters.  Basically, we play it as a sort of co-op, Fantasy version of "The Game of Life."  I sort the cards so that the different markets vaguely specialize in different products, and naturally we start with 4 items visible in each market from the get go.  Green and Yellow encounters are taken on solo, and then Blue and Red encounters are taken on as a team.  Throw in the Doom Track for a little bit of pressure, and the game is a decent RPG in board format.  Even with the Doom Track, though, the game still lacks pacing somehow,...honestly I think that "The Game of Life" has better pacing, but if I have to play it again I'll go nuts.  Too, we've never played a co-op all the way through, but it's a fun night every once in a while, and it scratches my fantasy game itch.  I even have this vague idea that one could create a cool story-oriented RPG experience by adding Descent for dungeons, assigning the deck expansions carefully (i.e., when story flavor warranted), and maybe use the "Sands of Kalim" as a neighboring desert (i.e., essentially have the boards side-by-side).  It'd be expensive, though, and require a lot of rule tweaking.

Update: Sands of Al-Kalim brought me back to this game, and it's really grown on me.  Works best, imo as a solitaire adventure game.  Bumped rating to 7

Update: Bumped rating to an 8 based not on the initial game, but on Runebound as a system.  Through the various expansions the system has continued to work quite well, and reaches a sort of apogee, I think, with Mists.  I haven't encountered any other game system that absorbs expansions so well.  Would rate higher, but I just don't think that the game plays that well beyond solo play.

Plus 3-4 hours for this game AND we play pretty fast.  The main thing that kills me is each player is playing their own game.  Player interaction is so low and you don't really care what happens to others on their turn.  WoW: TBG plays very similar.  I guess this could be good to bring out once a year but overall there are far too many better games that scratch this itch:  Descent, Doom, The last night on earth etc.  There is a huge item deck that i think i pulled from twice.  Yes, I only had 2 or 3 items (including allies) the whole game.  Events are fun with good flavor and SLOW build up of your characters powers is somewhat fun.  We played 3 XP per level.  I won't be buying this or playing it often but every once in awhile, maybe.

RATING: 6.5

Essentially it's a race game. So if you don't have another player to race against, you need to set a time limit and race against the clock (that's what Mr. Skeletor's threat-track solo rules do).
