=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Picked up the game recently after doing a bunch of research on various games that would be fun for solo or two players and I couldn't be happier with my choice.

By itself I'd rate the game an 8 or a 9 but the addition of so many expansions and ability to tweak the rules easily gives it the full 10.

I really enjoy the artwork, flavor text, and components as I do feel transported to a different land and like I'm going on an epic quest fraught with tension and peril. There's a lot of food to feed my imagination with the game and its a definite plus.

Setting it all up isn't too bad, especially if you organize all your bits and pieces. Once you're familiar with where all the adventure tokens go (which took all of one play) setting it up probably only takes a couple of minutes. Where everything goes is easy to figure out and there's enough room to fit everything comfortably on the board.

I've played the game solo at least a dozen times and while I admit that the base adventure does get kind of boring, I've been playing at least two hour stretches every day since I got it so take that with a grain of salt. 

Even just adding a small box expansion (in this case Avatars of Kelnov) somehow managed to change the feel of the game world entirely and with so many adventure variants to choose from its not difficult at all to fix this small issue.

I should also mention I've played exclusively with Mr Skeletor's threat track variant for solo play and its worked out wonderfully and I heartily recommend everyone who hasn't tried it, to at least check it out as having a variable timer is a great way to play.

I enjoy the movement dice system and playing with another player really heightens the tension. The first time I played with my friend I ended up winning quite literally because of ONE movement die and it was absolutely thrilling. That being said, it always sucks when you roll next to nothing useful, but I've found that if its happening to me, its also happening to the other player so it balances out. All in all I feel it adds to the game more than it detracts as I enjoy the tension it creates when you've suddenly got to really think about where you're going and how you're going to get there, lest you waste too much time.

I've read a lot of complaints about downtime between turns but its not been an issue thus far playing with two people. I can't say anything for or against with more players, but I can say the game is a phenomenal amount of fun with two people.

The only time the game got bogged down was in the very last turns of a Frozen Wastes expansion game where I had just found the princess and my friend had to try and slay me to prevent me from winning and we had to make sense of the PvP rules which honestly, aren't all THAT bad to figure out. It just took a few minutes of reading and re-reading the section. The other problem was there was no way for him to feasibly defeat me given how both our characters were built, but that wasn't the fault of the rules so much as circumstance.

PvP rules aside, everything runs smooth and makes sense and there's plenty of room for adaptation and tweaking so if you're looking for a race style of game play dressed up in an atmospheric and enjoyable fantasy setting then Runebound should fit the bill nicely. If you want a game you can play by yourself and have a blast then absolutely look no further.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Runebound also works fine as a single player game. I modified the 'Doom Track' rule for a single player; +1 card before a new Doom counter comes into play. This means that you add a new doom counter on the board every third card. That gives the game a better length. The regular Doom Track rules make the solo play way too short; you can only have nine cards at the doom track. There's no fun to the solitaire game this way because there really is too little time to develop your charachter.

Travel Hazard rules are also somewhat mandatory in solitary play. Withtout you're just running around the board without penalties for stopping on fields with no adventure counters on it. Travel hazard adds more tension.

Some encounter and adventure cards are useless in a solo game. (i.e. 'Give this card to another player, he then...'). 

A solo play expansion could be nice!

The encounters, events and challenges tell a story pretty well: the earlier adventures tell more of a threatening story about what is going to happen soon and later in the game the things get pretty grim. I like how some of the beaten monsters offer thematic rewards to use in further adventures. The battle system is really interesting and offers interesting choices with three different attack types and using the help of allies.

There are some things that could be improved about the game, but nothing too bad. The interaction is also on the low side, but it didn´t bother me for this kind of game. Probably plays best with 2-3 players.

RATING: 7.5
I own the second edition that came with the miniatures and revised rules.. thank god for that.

This is a good game for me and my friends - reason?  We're not grognards and enjoy a fun time.  This game (as purchased) is not broken, and like many other titles I own, should be appreciated for what it is - an experience game.  I think people who are creatively-minded will enjoy this more than those who aren't, because it is very easy to do anything you want.  I'll bet some people complain about the game 'out of the box', but you know, what Runebound and FFG provide you with is a backdrop to a realm, and its up to the players to bring their energy and excitement to the table.  Dice, chits, cards galore.  Good quality, much fun, and I'm looking forward to playing with the expansion.

Update:  Docked the game .5, relegating it to '7' territory.  As an experience game - it's good.  But.. it will take about 4 hours to result in a win.  For someone. Gets "meh"-ish after the 3rd hour.

(note: the timing here is due to the fact that I don't play with a regular gaming group, a lot of socializing is involved, as well as a lot of beer)

RATING: 9
OWN: 2nd EDITION + ALL EXPANSIONS
OWN: 3rd EDITION

2nd EDITION COMMENTS...

Awesome RPG on a board. It does take a while to play, but the adventures you get to have along the way make it well worth your time!

Compared with Return of the Heroes, Runebound tends to take longer but is meatier (more complex abilities & items etc.) and much more varied, while at the same time having much less convoluted rules, and being easier to tweak to your liking if required. It also has some of the feel of Talisman, but with many more choices to make.

Plays well with 2 players, and it's faster that way too.

Great components. The box is extremely over-sized, though in this case it could actually be useful for storing the expansions...

The HUGE number and variety of available expansions can only help the replayability (and the base game is hardly lacking in that area to begin with). Definitely a game that we'll be playing well into the future!

Some of the expansions encourage PvP combat, but the system doesn't really lend itself well to beating up on other characters. The base game seems to play better as a friendlier affair, although there are occasions where an attack on another player can be a good move, particularly if they're already weakened from an earlier combat.

NOTE: Includes unpainted grey figures (for player-characters only). With games like this I've recently taken the approach of blu-tacking small coloured poker chips underneath the figures (with a matching chip on the table in front of each player). This helps differentiate the figures when you don't have the time or desire to paint numerous figures.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
-The rules for this game are pretty vague in my opinion, it called for us to add some of our own rules for balancing. (Which I didn't mind so much, it made it costumizable.) 
-The movement system was different, but in a good way. 
-This game could use more interaction between players, (We added our own rules to make this happen.) other wise it's just a bunch of people playing by them selves at the same time. 
-I enjoyed the game, it was actually pretty fun to play, though it did last around 2-3 hours. It's nice if you want to kill an afternoon. Once you get the hang of the rules, it becomes enjoyable.
-I would like it if they re-did the fighting system a tad differently. Such as There is only need to defend and attack once per combat round.
-The final monsters became a bit too weak towards the end of the game. There has to be a level cap, and more exp needed to level up the higher you get.
-It would be nice if this game offered some lore as well, to make the role-playing aspect more doable.

All in all, this is a pretty decent game. It's fun and pretty customizable which I enjoyed. They could re-work a few things, but it's not that needed. If you see it on sale, or just for a price under 40 dollars, I 'd say buy it.

RATING: N/A
We played it just once. While it's a bit more complicated than, say, Talisman, it felt very dull and repetitive. Move, draw encounter card, fight (doing the same steps over and over again), advance to next "level". In Talisman, monsters are just a fraction of the events deck and you never know what you're going to run across. In Runebound, even if you draw something else, you still need to draw again just to complete the mandatory challenge card. It feels forced and not fun at all.

I'm not rating it because we didn't even finish the first game, but it's not something I would recommend to anyone - well, maybe to kids who love fantasy theme, although in my opinion Talisman delivers more laugh and with all the expansions, additional boards and stuff is just a superior adventure game in the "light thematic" category.

RATING: 5
I rate this game a 5 out of the box for 3 players.  It would be a point higher for 2 player or solitaire and a point lower for 4 players.  (It is unplayably slow with 5 or 6 players despite what the box says.)

The main problem with the game is the long play time and MANY people have suggested various house rules to speed the game up.  With a few simple house rules the game is much more fun.  Add 2 to the rating.

I've written a full review of the game which includes the house rules we like.

If you are going to enjoy this game do not play slow and cautious.  You are in a race, push the envelope and go after the tougher monsters quickly.  This both speeds the game and makes it more exciting. 

FFG fixes a number of problems in this costly game in an expensive big box expansion.  (Like what they have done in TI3 and Game of Thrones board game.)  If you want to encourage such a publisher strategy, then buy this game and the $60 expansions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]Traded away May 2012[/b]

I enjoyed this game solo using Mr. Skeletor's threat track rules as well as with 2 players using some modified co-op type rules for a while. 

[b]What I like[/b]:
:d10-1: It has a strong RPG flavor. I liked the character building even though it was limited to three character traits.
:d10-2: I appreciate the art on the cards and the flavor and theme the cards impart on the game.
:d10-3: The combat mechanism is cool. Resolve distance, magic and then melee attacks. Because of this, each character approaches combat differently and has strengths and weaknesses that others don't.
:d10-4: Each character has special abilities that further differentiate them.

[b]What I don't like[/b]:
:d10-1: The tension in the multiplayer game doesn't match the theme. For a theme where heroes are trying to stop an evil force trying to dominate the land, Runebound is really a race against other hero players, and not a competition against the bad guys. The bad guys in this game don't apply any pressure against the good guys. They are just there to provide the players something to compete over. In my perfect world, this would have been a cooperative game and the players would be working together to defeat the enemy who would apply pressure and possibly defeat the players.
:d10-2: My main reason for purchasing this game was to have a solitaire RPG game which would immerse me in theme and provide a challenging game, as all great solitaire titles do. I think there are some good variants out there that approach this, but they ultimately didn't quite cut it for me. I also see potential for personally developing a homemade variant to suit my needs. However, it seemed like too much work when there are other games out there that are enjoyable without a lot of work from me. 
:d10-3: No tension, whether multiplayer or solitaire. I need tension, and what this game provides does not suffice.

[b]The Main Point[/b]
Ultimately I think FFG missed the boat with this one by not developing Runebound as a cooperative with the enemy able to exert a competitive game against the players. I guess I'm really looking for an Arkham Horror type game in a standard fantasy setting. I found more or less what I'm looking for in Defenders of the Realm, although Runebound has some elements I like that aren't in DotR. I can definitely understand this game working for some players, especially those with the right group. But it just didn't work for me as a solitaire or multiplayer game.

RATING: 3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.1
Solo Player Value = Fair.  A good game, but better with more players.  More players adds the element of speed and necessity to the game.

Two Player Value = Good. A fun game that will get played more.

Three Player Value = Good. A fun game that will get played more. 


Runebound is an adventure game where players take on the role of heroes who stomp across the land to fulfill a pre-subsrcibed task of killing the bad guy. To help them on their travels the players can hire allies and obtain powerful weapons, armor and items. Characters also level and get more powerful as they fight against the baddies.

That is basically the same plot and mechanic of every role-playing video game ever made. So I find it helpful to think of Runebound as a board game version of a RPG video game. Same concept minus the controller.

Overall Runebound is a fun game to play and it handles the role-playing aspect very well. You feel like you are in a role-playing game.

The board/map is large and filled with many adventure sites. Plenty enough to keep to players busy. There are also many cities the players can visit to acquire good and companions.

Movement of the characters across the board/map is handled by a well designed dice mechanic. Players will need to learn how to take advantage of proper movement to make their character go where they want.

Encounters are clearly marked on the board/map and are labeled with a level of difficultly, or experience level, so players know which encounters best suit their character. This very helpful when leveling your character so you know which encounters offer more experience. This is again very helpful for the end game so you know which encounters to go after to find the bad guy.

The actual encounter is handled through a deck of cards that represent that level of difficultly or experience. Draw the top and play along.

Leveling your hero is very simple. Each encounter gives experience in the form of currency equal to the experience level. Players use that currency to purchase upgrades for the heroes that will help them in a specific way; like better melee fighting or more hit points.

Buying items and allies is equally easy. Characters can visit cities and purchase any available items with the money they loot from killing monsters.

Combat uses an interesting 2d10 system. This offers the player better odds when rolling for hitting and killing enemies than using a single d20. Modifying the roll is simply stacking all the bonuses from weapons and items.

The components are all very good. The board/map contains a lot of information yet remains clean and easy to read. The hexes on the map have nice artwork and look a lot the real environment. The cards are made from a heavy stock and have well done art as well.

When we start talking about strategy is where Runebound takes a few knocks because there is not much here. Roll, move, fight. Repeat. It feels almost puzzle like. In fact, it feels just like an RPG video game. Run around looking for things to kill so you can level enough to kill the boss.

Runebound also offers players a different strategy aspect with the ability to attack each other. I never enjoyed this much as it does nothing to progress to the game. Nor does it offer experience for your character. I prefer to play the game as a multi-player solitaire.

Two suggested variants are helpful to use. One is a timing mechanic to bring on the end game quicker to prevent players from stalling. The other is used to make traveling more adventurous and adds in random encounters when moving. I enjoy and use both.

I usually do not like mentioning expansions while discussing the base game, but I will make an exception in this case. The game handles expansions extremely well. Many expansions exists for Runebound and they come in two types: Big box and small box. Big box expansions offer new maps and whole new adventures. Small box expansions only change the existing game slightly. I prefer small box expansions. I believe it better to alter the base game just slightly, to tweak it a little, as opposed to changing it entirely.


Pros - 
- True RPG video game feel 
- Easy to learn and play 


Cons -
- Not must strategy
- The expansions are hard to find and very costly 



Overall Playability = 7.1

Design = 6
Strategy = 6
Luck = 8
Artwork & Components = 7
Use of Mechanics = 8
Time of Play = 7
Fun = 7
Innovation = 6
Analysis Paralysis = 8
Theme = 8


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


As mentioned in the full write-up of the game, Runebound handles expansions fantastically.  The expansions fit beautifully into the game.

Also mentioned above were the different types of expansions: Big Box and Small Box.

I do not like the big box expansions. I feel they alter the base game too greatly. The big box expansions offer a new board/map with a whole new adventure. Big box expansions also give you new characters for the new adventure. Plus new weapons, armors, items and allies. Basically, it is an entirely new game. Not much of an expansion really.  It could be a new stand alone game except it requires the components from the base game.

I prefer the small box expansions. These expansions only change the base game slightly. They change or add-to the base in some small detail. 

Additionally, small box expansions come in several different types. Each type of small box expansion changes or adds to the game in a certain way. The different types of small box expansions are:
- [i]Item & Allies[/i] - Gives the players more powerful items
- [i]Challenge[/i] - Adds to the adventure cards stacks to bring new challenges in encounters
- [i]Adventure[/i] - Changes the base adventure to a whole new adventure while using the same base game 
- [i]Character[/i] - Gives new characters to use

Of the four types of small box expansions I only use two kinds: Challenge and Adventure. They offer the biggest change to the game while still keeping the base game intact.

I will go over each small box expansion briefly below while breaking them into their types.


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]

Item & Allies expansions are simply more items and extra allies that are added to the deck.

Many of the added items and allies are extremely powerful.

A few of these are a lot of fun to play with, however I do not use any of them.


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Character expansions add new abilities to the characters.

Each expansions add a new class that can be added to the character that provide new and powerful abilities.  Each has new cards to power the added abilities.

Some of these are fun to play with, but I don't use any of them.


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Challenge expansions add cards to the adventures decks to make the encounters more difficult or to add a new twist in the game.

These types of expansions can be used in conjunction with other types of expansions. You can even use multiple challenge expansions together. But I prefer not to.

At most I enjoy the addition a single challenge expansions and a single Adventure expansion.

The Challenge expansions I have are - 



[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


Adventure expansions alter the end game condition.  These take out the existing end bad guy and replace it with completely new win conditions.

I really enjoy types of expansions because they bring a new feeling to the game yet retain the basics.

I do enjoy use a single adventure explains in conjunction with a single challenge expansion.

The adventure expansions that I have are - 

- [thing=18623][/thing]
- [thing=23510][/thing]
- [thing=23511][/thing]
- [thing=28875][/thing]

RATING: 7
Runebound is quite fun.  I very rarely solo game, but Runebound is excellent for that using Mr. Skeletor's threat track rules.  I scratches the fantasy gaming itch.  

I also play this with my 2 boys, who love it.  The challenge with Runebound for me is that it takes a long time to play.  That is not so much a problem playing solo with threat track rules.  But when playing with the kids it can take a while.  We use modified characters that start the game supercharged in abilities and each starts with one random item and ally, each a cost of 6 or under.  We also allow healing of 2 hearts for 1 gold.  Using these tweaks, we take a 6 hour game and get it down to 90 to 120 mins.  For an extra short game, we play what we call Dragon Hunt.  Under that variant the first player to succeed against a red threat wins.   Anyway, we enjoy the game and the kids have fun creating new characters (we developed a set of character development rules as well) with special abilities and the like.  Runebound does not scale as easily as, say, Prophecy.  Prophecy is very easily scaled and for that reason better for us.  But we still enjoy RB a lot and I expect we will be getting some of the big board and other expansions over the years.  

EDIT:  With more experience, our games are shorter and we are using the base characters who start with a few enhancements and a cheap ally and item.  We play different end game scenarios depending on how much time we have.  Sometime we play to one red kill, sometimes the normal scenario end, and sometimes we just have a real world time limit and use a self-made score system.  It is all very enjoyable.  My initial rating for this was about a 6.5, it later moved up to an 8.  It was at a 9 for awhile but is back down to an 8.

RATING: 9
Played solo using threat track variant. Probably took 6 hours to finish. Might play with 2 players, definitely not with more. Would prefer if the game were cooperative. Movement dice can be a nuisance when you're trying to get somewhere and they won't let you. Most of the time its okay but, near the end, most Red challenges are in forest; if you don't roll a forest, you need to wait another turn and keep trying till you do (which, technically, might never happen). Definitely worth investing in things that aid movement (the Teleport rune, for instance, is excellent). Each time you defeat a Red challenge it becomes easier to defeat the next, not sure that's a good thing. The map is not especially interesting. It would be better if the towns had more differentiation/specialization, to encourage you to visit them for specific reasons. Will try "Cities of Adventure" fan-made variant to see if that helps. Also need to play with the adventure variant next time, so traveling becomes more interesting. Can see this becoming stale without expansions. Consider getting the desert and ice expansions....

RATING: 7
There are many valid complaints one might have about this game. There's the multiplayer solitaire factor. There's the lack of meaningful decisions to make. There's the samey nature of gameplay - you hardly ever do anything else than fight monsters and go to town to heal and buy items. The storyline is a (possibly intentional) turkey, and I hardly ever felt immersed into it.

Those are all qualities of a bad game. Yet somehow, in the end, it's not so bad. In fact, it's very addictive - for some reason that I can't put my finger on, I keep wanting to play this thing over and over again.

You're going to need to buy expansions if you intend to play this game more than once or twice. Otherwise, you'll just be fighting the same old monsters every game you play. No need to go overboard though, even if it's tempting because they're so cheap - two or three challenge decks make a lot of difference.

RATING: 6.5
I purchased this specifically for solitaire play, and I've squeezed many many hours of solid entertainment from it.  My first half-dozen plays or so were very fun, but now that I've seen most of what the base game (and some of the expansions) has to offer, I've lost a lot of interest. The combat system is elegant, and there's definitely a sense of satisfaction that comes from building up your hero and collecting powerful items. However, ultimately the game just comes down to a rather dull move-attack-heal pattern with little strategic consideration.  It desperately needs a quest system.

Once I added the "Sands" big-box expansion, the fundamental weaknesses of the base game really stood out.  With Sands, it is a MUCH stronger game (see my review of Sands for more details) but Sands has limited replayability due to lack of expansions.  I wish Runebound was more than it is because in many ways, this is the game I dreamt about when i was a 10 year old boy.

RATING: 8.5
We have probably spent more time as a family (not the wife :( ) playing this game (with variants) than any other.  It does run a little long, but using some tricks keeps the downtime minimized.  

Leveling up and overland adventure is just fantastic.  No game does that better.  It isn't super-strategic, but it does require you to push your luck and take some risks.  And excellent, well-developed theme throughout!

My kids (7 and 10) love it.  If it was an hour we would play it more, but then it wouldn't be as epic and your character wouldn't grow, so i think it is the perfect length as is.

Definitely not recommended with more than 3 total players, and 2 is better in many ways.

RATING: 5
Generic fantasy game of heroes wandering about, fighting monsters and getting loot.

I vaguely recall reading that Martin Wallace came up with the core system for the game then handed it over to Fantasy Flight to flesh out, which doesn't surprise me. The movement mechanism in particular is unusual and interesting (if absurdly unthematic); the flavour text and everything else is as dull as can be.

I'm pretty sure that it's actually best as a solitaire game, since additional players add essentially no interaction, merely a great deal of downtime. It's basically a race to victory and the same tension can be achieved by playing the game on a timer using the doom track.

If the downtime and lack of interaction aren't a concern -- if you sanely choose to play it only solitaire, for example -- then it's a very solid entry in the "more story than strategy" genre. Mechanically it all works well and there's a real narrative progression in the strength of the monsters and the ability of the characters. My problem is mostly that, as with the rest of its genre, I can't see a reason for it to exist -- you're better off playing a real RPG for an immersive storytelling experience or a strategy game for some actual strategy.

I enjoy it more than my rating would suggest, but I can't see ever wanting to get it to the table. Sold my copy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
This started out fun, but just takes much too long for what it is. After a while you're just rolling dice, rolling some more dice, then rolling some more dice. Once behind it's difficult to catch up, which makes the game duration even more of a problem.

Besides that, I strongly dislike the dice-based movement system. It has several problems: it can be quite hard to figure out what to do with your roll, adding to the time you need to take your turn; and since you don't know what you're going to roll, it's hard to anticipate. Also, it's extremely frustrating to not get anywhere because of a bad roll.

In short - not my adventure board game. I'm still to find mine, incidentally.

RATING: 7
Runebound is the best Ameritrash adventure game I've played.  Mage Knight is better, but that's a totally different animal.  The WoW Adventure game is similar, but not nearly as good.

There are a ton of different heroes and they all play very differently.  I really like the leveling progression and how you can choose which stats to increase.  The items were fun and varied, the encounters were entertaining, and the combat system is unique but satisfying.  The movement system also provided some interesting puzzle solving and unpredictability.

The game can be very lucky/swingy, but that's not uncommon for the genre.  There can also be a lot of downtime because you have nothing to do on other people's turns, so I think the game is best with 2 players.  I would maybe try a 3 player game, but 4 would be way too long.  I haven't looked at the solo rules, but this would be a fun solo game.

RATING: 8
Ah, I LOVED this game. So much my friends had to have an intervention. No joke.

This game has so much variation, from encounters, to characters, to leveling builds, to strategies, to item/ally drops. Just with the base set, you can play and replay it to a point of obscenity before it stops feeling fresh, and that's without even involving expansions. 

All that said, I mentioned an intervention? This is absolutely a "winning strategy" game... it involves a lot of adapting to the circumstances, but at any given time, there is probably one decision you can make over the others that is actively wiser and more likely to lead to success... and the player who has the most knowledge of the game will thus win more often than not. Eventually it got to a point where out of the couple dozen people I played with, I only had two friends who had a chance of beating me, and one who'd never won once and was stubbornly determined to beat me at least once... other than them, basically all the rest of my friends sat me down and told me I always win and that's no fun and they never wanted to play again.

So... buyer beware... depending on your skill level, you may become so profficient no one will play you, or alternately you may end up finding a friend/friends who you can never beat.

RATING: 10
This has to be the most well-designed game I've ever played.The game maintains a balance of skill and luck throughout every element of play. Every decision you make has a meaningful outcome on the situation and there is little chance for the cards to favor one player over another. Draw a tough monster? Get a higher reward for slaying it. Draw a nice item card? Pay extra for it. Everything given usually must be earned, but rarely punished.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
First Impression:
Here's a game I never thought I'd like.

It's a fantasy adventure game, with heroes that roam the land slaughtering the "terryfying" but practically helpless mosters left and right gaining exp until someone manages to beat the boss and win the game. It's terribly simple and straightforward but that is a strong point in a fully thematic game. It's enjoyable and keeps players interested.

Despite my detest for fantasy themes and luck-full no-decisions ameritrash games, I'd rate this game even higher - that's how much I liked it. However, there is one serious, almost fatal flaw, that doubles the duration of the game, adds pointless downtime and tires the players for no reason: The movement mechanism. I'm speechless about it. Rolling dice for movement is terrible to begin with, but rolling dice and then trying to figure out where you can move is 10 times worse. I seriously encourage homeruling a simple point cost movement against this abomination (5 MPs to spend per turn + 1 for every henchman/object that helps movement by rerolling and roads/cities 1MP cost, plains/rivers 2MP cost, hills 3MP cost, woods/mountains/swamps 3MP cost or something like that). This simple rule will speed the game to a full gallop and allow the players to focus on the meat of the game - the adventuring.

Overall I expect my rating to drop the more I play this game as I expect to progressively get bored by it. As it is now however, I am eager to play it again, escpecially with the movement homeruled.

RATING: N/A
This game seems to go on forever. I play it a lot actually - because my wife and I have found that it is a great game to have standing somewhere and do a couple of encounters once in a while. I do highly recommend making it easier to gain levels. The game has a suggestion for quicker play to reduce the needed experience points to 4 for 2 people. I prefer lowering it even further. Something I do like is that the game gets some "tradeable cardgame feel" as you try to figure out how to best combat an encounter. I have been very impressed by how, if you press on without waiting too much, each battle can seem very close and dangerous, yet you rarely end up dying -- which is a good gamemaster's job -- although there is no gamemaster here. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Great game to play on a lazy Sunday, to relive the old school days of boardgaming. When you are in the mood for a bit of a luck fest and don't mind spending some time with a game, this is quite a good title with great quality components (love the artwork!). Personally, I like this and Return Of The Heroes about the same, albeit for different reasons. Both are better than the much overrated Talisman, although the second edition of that game remains charming for its artwork alone. Runebound looks better, has better written rules and more variety than ROTH. Return Of The Heroes, however, is faster with less text to read and less downtime between turns (mainly because it requires les micro management). But be warned: All games of this ilk seem to have very limited player interactivity. It is much easier to settle for Return Of The Heroes for a similar experience.
UPDATE: It has been a couple of years after my first play and I recently played it again with two players. I like the micro management of henchmen and equipment quite a lot. The looks are not as attractive as back then, though, because lately FFG and DOW have been turning out much better looking titles. The only drawback of this game is that it is very, very long. It took us 5 hours to finish it! I think it will not see the table more than maybe once a year because of that.

RATING: 7
Runebound fills the light fantasy niche for me just about as well as I could hope. The rules are simple enough to explain in just a few minutes, the combat and movement systems provide enough choices to be interesting without provoking paralysis, and there's enough story in the cards to be entertaining. Like most games of this style, it can run a bit long, especially with more players - I don't think I'd want to play this with more than three. And there's certainly lots of luck involved - I wouldn't play this as a "serious" game in the same way that I would Magic Realm. But Runebound makes for a fine afternoon's entertainment when I'm in the right mood for it. It also solos extremely well.

RATING: N/A
Darrell Hardy & Martin Wallace; This plays well solo. In this game you take the role of a character which is one of several adventurers. You travel around over the world looking to find adventures which are presented on the board as dots. Once you land on a dot, you draw a card that tells you what monster (normally it's a monster that you fight, and that's the adventure) you battle, or on rare occasions, what events take place. Upon defeating the monster, or finishing the quick adventure, you gain experience dependant on how high a level the monster was. In the basic game without any expansions, you face one major world threat, which once you defeat it, or it defeats you, the game ends. Lots of expansions!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It's Fantasy Flight, so not surprisingly the components are a highlight. High quality cards and lots of them, plastic miniatures for the heroes, countless heavy duty tokens and a nicely illustrated gameboard. The only downside is the dice, which comparatively speaking are pretty cheap. They are stickered, and in an odd design choice, illustrated with symbols lifted right out of a dungeons and dragons campaign map circa 1978. Luckily, I am just lame enough to recognize them, so it wasn't tremendously difficult for me to interpret them. Still, normal people may require a cheat sheet the first couple of times through.

At first glance, Runebound seems like a good light roleplaying game in the vein of Arkham Horror or Last Night on Earth. In truth, what Runebound delivers is multi-player solitaire. Player turns are completely non-interactive, to the point that a player with a second set of dice can take their turn simultaneously with the active player. While this certainly can speed things up, it detracts from the immersion that games of this nature require to be successful.

Another challenge to the game's atmosphere is that despite its outward appearance as a fantasy adventure game, Runebound is actually a race game...albeit a very slow one. The only impetus to act is the aggressiveness of the other players. If they all play too cautiously (and they often will, as character death is brutally punishing) the game can drag on endlessly. I have seen sessions which should have ended in two hours instead go almost three and a half because the leader didn't want to risk dying, especially when he had such a commanding lead. This sort of end game stalemate happens fairly often, allowing the game to fall into a rut if no one is willing to push it forward. It my experience, once a leader is established the competitive game is over. From that point forward, the game turns into a cooperative effort with everyone working together to help the leader win...just so they can get it over with already.

Mechanically, the game is solid, but certainly not ground breaking. It probably would have been better served as a straight co-op game with a strong group nemesis. As it sits, with nothing to really drive the game to conclusion and no real player interaction, it all just seems rather long and pointless.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Runebound is a good solitaire game. The Base game has stronger theme (compared with the expansions). The Event, Challenge and Encounter cards do very well telling the story!
Considering, you play with Mr. Skeletor's Solo rules, it works perfect solo! See here:

http://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/49058/runebound-solo-rules

Also, for more story elements, I recommend to play with the Cities of Adventure expansion. Thus each town gets the unique backgroud and events happening during you visit the town. (But it makes the game longer).

[b]Pro[/b]:
1. Rules: Easy to learn and to play.

2. Very good fantasy adventure theme, which is well supported by the mechanics, abilities and card images.

3. Difficulty is good scalable, using the Threat Track   solo.

4. Nice card images and flavour text.

[b]Contra[/b]:
1. Usually my solo game takes 8-10 hours (with the Cities of Adventure expansion), because I am still playing at low Threat difficulty.
1.1 Improvements/House rules:
- I play the expansions with the Faster Experience Levelling variant rule from the Frozen Wastes Expansion rulebook, p. 14. 

2. Some item abilities feel at first a bit unthematic, but if you read the following thread, you might see this from an other point of view:

http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/579724/activation-question/new

3. The rules aren't written very well, so I often have to look up a specific rule in the FAQ. But the BGG rule forum helped me also quite a lot!

RATING: 7
A medium-complex adventure/role-playing game. It adds good variety to a collection. The game is largely individualistic -- each player is on their own quest upgrading their characters. Thus, there could be big downtime as each player does their stuff. The game seems to work well with 3, but would be horrible with 5-6.

There is luck involved, since the battles are dice based and the items/monsters you encounter come from shuffled decks. It seems like a decent straightforward adventure game, and I'd have to play again to make a further assessment. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Well I bought this game together with Dungeon Twister. I read numerous articles here on BGG about adventure type games. My collection is heavenly euro/german orientated. So I wanted something different...

I've played Runebound 2nd Edition twice now, solo. And I liked it! The rules didn't have many problems for me. I found them very clear. The only questions I had were about combat situations and the activation of cards.

I found one typo in the rules; a hero can use his special before combat ability once, while on page 8 of the rules "Example of a challenge" it appears a hero can use it more often. As always this answer is covered here on BGG. Some other questions I had were all covered here on BGG too.

What did I like about this game:
- The game tells a story, if you can use you’re imagination, you're wandering the Runebound world.
- At first I had a dice rolling feeling, and I don't like dice... But soon I lost this feeling, for example using dice to move is (for me) something completely new, and feels innovative!
- You can't just jump around and pick up adventures and start beating your opponents. You have to upgrade your hero and use allies, weapons, armour etc. Which all can be bought at the market, the good ones cost hard earned gold! Loved that too; earn it hard and use it wise! Upgrading takes a while, but gives great satisfaction.
- So this game has a strategic touch; upgrade in the right way, choose your allies wise, pick the right items etc.
- In other words I felt myself get more involved and attached to my hero.
- The combat system is just great. Most games I've played that use dice for combat solutions didn't give a feeling of control. After playing for example "Struggle of Empires" I discovered that dice can be conquered. In Runebound it's the same; if you upgrade and use your hero and his abilities wise, if you choose the right allies etc. you’ll stand a change. That makes the combat very strategic, because it has enough 'layers' to choose from! It gives the game tension.
- This game got me involved on an emotional level; good games do this with me.

I won't rate the game yet. Must play it more often and keep the feeling I've described here above.

Conclusion; I bought the game with reservations, at first I was afraid that it would be a dice game without enough strategic options. Two games solo until now proved this feeling wrong. It has enough strategic options where you can control the dice.

After my third (solo or not to solo) game I'll come back. For the record; this is the first game that I've played twice solo and finished.

RATING: N/A
A fantasy/adventure board game where a particular adventure is played. Each adventure determines the object of the quest, and what wonders and obstacles will be encountered along the way.
Players take turns navigating a board that displays the terrain of a fantasy world. Each turn allows a character to visit towns or friendly locations (where items and allies can be found), facing an encounter (which provide events and enemies), or interacting with another player by landing in the same space.

RATING: 9
This is the solo and multi-adventure game i've been looking for. With Sands of Al Kalim gameplay improvements I actually prefer it to Talisman and even, dare I write, the new D&D games. I think it's the bright, old-skool card art and lush hills and mountains on the board that give it the edge for me over the damp caverns of D&D's Drizzt and Co.

Admittedly, the game needs some rules tweaks:

For a game clock I use Skeletor's doom track for solo. I also strongly recommend Judd Jensen's Cities of Adventure die roll system (see files section under variants for all these tweaks) which adds oodles of theme; Also, the Frozen Wastes' XP system - level up by paying 1 more than current level - ensures a fast-track start but no runaway leader (a problem with standard base rules).

RB would benefit from the 'daily, at will and utility' battle card system of D&D's excellent Drizzt and 'Loft, but RB has plenty of its own whizzo weapons and special items than can be activated in battle in a not too dissimilar fashion, especially if you get a few weapon and item add-on packs.

RATING: 7
***Update***

Like many competitive dungeon crawlers, the game works well as a 2-player game.  For each player beyond the second, knock two points off the rating due to downtime issues.

***Original Review***

Thirty minutes into our four-hour game, it was obvious that only one player had any chance of winning.  (Me, it turns out, but that didn't make the game any more fun.)

Another race-to-kill game that is much less kid-friendly - in terms of complexity, downtime, and player versus player combat - than Return of the Heroes.

If I ever play Runebound again, it will not be with more than three players.  And I'll definitely be using the fast "level up" rules.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]BRILLIANT RPG-LITE ADVENTURE GAME.[/b]

I traded away Risk:LOTR for this big-boxed beauty, so it HAD to be good. And how!

I wanted an adventure game similar to Talisman, et al, that would allow players to explore a fantasy game-world in boardgame form. This is that game. The hunt has been called off!
On the climb up Geek Mountain, this is one of the wayposts.

The graphic design on the cards, and the artwork itself, is up there with the very best in CCGs. The board is thick, sturdy, and functional; a little more fancy touches on the board would have been nice though - but it's no biggy. The components are uniformly excellent, and the box big enough to fill with other bits and bobs too, like expansion packs, etc.
The rules are clear, with examples; nothing is that complicated for a hardened gamer.
The card and dice driven systems work very well. The movement dice are certainly unique, and try their best to simulate the sometimes frustrating holdups you get with a hike in the wilderness.
Combat is handled particularly effectively, and more intricately than I was expecting; a multi-phase affair with allies jumping into the fray, runes cast, large broadswords swung, and artifacts/abilities used. This leads to multiple decisions by the player on any given turn. It is also one of the superior facets of the game, lifting the life-or-death melees well above the likes of Talisman, Dungeon, and the Warlock of Firetop Mtn.

Some say that the central quest maybe a tad hackneyed.
So what?
You get to travel a strange land, sword in hand, looking for villainous dragons to slay - that's how I want an adventure game to pan out for pity's sake!
If you are looking for variety, other expansions with alternate endgames can be bought.
Crucially, this game is FUN. A great deal of geeky pleasure can be wrought from forging a hero from humble beginnings, and then wielding her with a firm hand once she has become all-powerful. Just like in real life. Ha!

It should be noted, however, that the game atmosphere is enormously enhanced, if the players read the flavour text on the cards to each other. This contributes  to the game's storytelling aspect instead of the game merely being a card-flipping, dice-rolling excercise, and transforms the experience into a Fighting Fantasy book style experience. The truth being, that if you're not in the mood to descend into the spirit of Runebound, you might as well not bother getting the box down from the shelf.

Timewise, the game trots along with 1/2 players, but really starts to grind at 3+. At this point my score would drop to a solid 8 or a droopy 7, players depending.

[b]As a rule of thumb, deduct 2 points from my rating for each player beyond the first.[/b]

[b]GAME-HACKER TIPS:[/b]

1. Ditch the Doomtrack rules - use a house rule.
2. Replace/paint the figures.
3. Replace Doomtrack tokens with pewter skulls.
4. Replace the coins with ersatz gold.
5. Replace XP tokens with glass pebbles.
6. Ban PvP duelling.

Now you're ready to eviscerate dragonkind with the very best of us.

All in all, this game does everthing that could be asked of it, and then a little bit more. I would certainly not turn down a game, and heartily advise all who like this sort of thing to buy it.
Runebound also plays marvellously well solo.

I own a ridiculous number of card expansions for this game!

Quite likely the most customizable board game out there, Runebound suffers from being a very slow game, taking hours- even days- to finish, with serious downtime between turns. While the individual elements are nothing too creative, overall the game presents itself well.

Despite its length, a favorite of my gaming groups.
